E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|The dissertation proposes five assumptions as a basisfor investigating the deterioration and conservation of bibliographic resources in Nigerian Libraries. The assumptions are:1)	In Nigeria, as elsewhere in the world, the causes of bibliographic deterioration could be traced to the following factors:(a)	Inherent,(b)	External, and(c)	Structural.2) Conservation should form part of the core professicnalduties of librarians in Nigeria.3) Practising librarians in Nigeria do not have pertinent qualification and adequate experience in conservation work,4) The resources available for conservation work inNigerian libraries are grossly inadequate.5) In Nigeria, librarians are mostly concerned with the orthodox professional duties of bibliographic collection, organisation and dissemination.The assumptions were tested by means of a questionnaire designed and sent to the library under study and which was filled and returned. Thereafter, a personal,on the spot observation of facts, resources and facilities was undertaken coupled with unstructural random interview of some of the library staff, It was found that the causative agents of deterioration of bibliographic resourcesare: (1) acidity in paper during the manufacturing processes(2)	environmental factors - particularly those associated with temperature, light, humidity, theft insects and flood;(3)	structural factors due mostly to usage and handling.However, the study revealed that though librarians in the library are fully aware of the need for untiring conservation work in libraries, their current approach to the effort is not quite modern due mainly to many inadequacies such as the lack of good knowledge of modern scientific conservation themselves and insufficient resources and facilities. While these librarians do have a good high quality academic and librarianship qualifications; and most do have sufficient professional and working experience none (at least in the staff of University of Ife library) has a recognised pertinent  training and qualification in the field of bibliographic conservation, Certainly, this is a reflection of most, if not all, of Nigerian libraries since a University library demands the best of the highly qualified professional staff in a nation's library service.The facilities or resources for conservation work are insufficient for reasonable work and efforts to be done in the field of bibliographic conservaion. The study clearly shows that there is hardly any conservation laboratory or workshop as well as the absence of disaster or contigency plans designed for conservation effort.Consequently, conservation works at the moment depend on the conventional individual room airconditioning facilities and binding which are sparsely used in conjunction with daily cleaning and proper shelving of resources and the emphatic express wish to do something more positive in no distant future.However, by way of general overview, Nigeriansituation with regards to the causative agents of deterioration is similar to those in advanced countries of the world where enormous scientific experiments have been done on the phenomenon. As a positive measure to follow the foot steps of the advanced countries, the dissertation ends with a suggestion for a national body to be known as Nigerian Association for Bibliographic Conservation (N A B C ) whose primary duty will be the prolongationof the life of bibliographic resources in Nigerian libraries.|Theses collection|Deterioration and conservation of bibliographic resources/Bibliographic resources in Nigerian Libraries/Nigerian Libraries*||||||Afolayan Samuel Sunday|Male|Lakeside Resort, Asa Dam, Ilorin  "Home of Tourists (Leisure, Business and Educational) in Ilorin and its environs||||||M. E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|The study was designed to determine the extent of adoption of five rubber innovations in Bendel State. These are (1) the use of trained tappers and the tappers training scheme at the Rubber Research Institute of Nigeria, (2) adoption of bud and or clonal rubber, (3) adoption of rubber replanting scheme, (4) use of ethrel and (5) the adoption of the aid scheme of the Nigerian Rubber Board.A total of one hundred and twenty five (125) rubber farmers were interviewed with the aid of structured interview schedule and the analysis used was frequencies and percentages. The result of the analysis showed that 70.4 to 95.2 percent of the respondents claimed awareness of at least one of the rubber innovations. On the other hand, 19.2 to 69.6 percent have tried at least one of the innovations and 16.8 to 68.8 percent of the respondents have fully adopted at least one of the innovations.The factors identified, which contributed to adoption of these innovations by farmers were contact with extension agents and radio, cosmopoliteness of farmers, characteristics of the innovations and farmers membership of co-operative societies.Other factors mentioned, which militated against adoption of rubber innovations included lack of labour, high cost of production, lack of credit, lack of input	and uncooperative attitude of some officers.The conclusions were (i) that three out of the five rubber innovations were highly adopted. These were the use of trained tappers; adoption of bud and or clonal rubber; and use of the aid scheme of the Nigerian Rubber Board. (ii) Mass media was found to be inadequately used, except for radio in a single innovation. (iii) The extension personnel of the Ministry of Agriculture were largely responsible for the dissemination of research findings on rubber and (iv) there is a high level of awareness (70.4 to 95.2 percent) relative to low adoption of 16.8 to 68.8 percent.The recommendations included (i) provision of creditto rubber farmers for replanting; (2) increased utilisation of mass media, through organised radio listening forum by extension agents; (3) strengthening the extension division of the Ministry of Agriculture for effective dissemination of research findings to rubber farmers and provision of inputs on regular and timely basis.|Theses collection|Agricultural innovations/Rubber innovations/Rubber farmers*||||||Adamolekun Ladipo Olu|Male|Administrative Science in Francophone West Africa||||||M. Phil. Administration|M. Crowder, D. J. Murray||Ile - Ife, Nigeria|Obafemi Awolowo University, |1970|||OAU|||||122p.|||||||English||Obafemi Awolowo University, H. O. Library, Ile-Ife, Osun State. E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|Starting with a discussion of what conception of Administrative science exists in France, this study examines the influence of this French conception on developments in the same field in francophone West Africa. The study reveals that all francophone West Africa accept the existence of an Administrative science largely because of their common contact with the French conception through colonial experience  a contact the French themselves have nurtured and promoted by providing both human and material resources for the creation of special schools charged with its dissemination. From a case study of three representative special schools we come to the conclusion that there are two conceptions of Administrative Science in Francophone west Africa both of which are built around three strands of courses inherited from France: general culture courses, specialization courses and professional training. One of these conceptions adopts most of the French content, adapting only certain aspects of it to suit local realities. This is illustrated by two of our case studies  the Parisbased Francophone School of Administration(Chapter II) and the Senegalese National School of Administration (Chapter III)  with the latter shoving greater adaptation than the former. Our third case study reveals how Guinea, by considering content as a reflection of clearly defined national training objectives, has, through its revolutionary political philosophy, radically modified all three strands of the French content to produce a distinctively Guinean conception of Administrative science. The organization and development of the representative schools are discussed in Chapters II, III and IV and attention is drawn to both their achievements and problems. Our conclusion is that francophone West Africa has benefited from its belief in Administrative Science but each country is likely to produce its own distinct conception.|Theses collection|Francophone West Africa/Administrative science*||||||Adisa Folajinmi Olabode|Male|From Jerusalem to Camp David the Middle-East Peace Process.||||||M. Within three decades, it has seen five wars and vast ravages and destruction. Moreover, its special mark in International Relations has been the conflict and enimity existing between the Arabs and Israelis living in the region. The conflict has, particularly in 1973, threatened to involve the superpowers, i.e. the US and USSR in direct military confrontation with catastrophic consequences for the whole world. This background underlines the importance of the period from Jerusalem to Camp David and makes it worthy of study.This period (covering from November 1977 to March 1979) witnessed a remarkable peace-process in the Middle-East. The peace-process which was set in motion by Egyptian President Anwar el Sadat's trip to Jerusalem eventually resulted in a peace treaty between Egypt and Israel, thus ending the state of war that had existed between them for thirty years.This dissertation undertakes a study of the development of this phenomenon. The study begins by analysing the motivations of the Sadat initiative. It then proceeds to examine and account for the reactions of other states to the initiative and the consequent developments leading up to the Camp David Accords in September 1978. Furthermore, it looks at the content of the Accords and the period between it and the signing of the Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty in March 1979. In this respect, it attempts to identify the nature and sources of the problems that delayed the process. Sc. Agricultural Economics|Yakub L. Fabiyi||Ile-Ife, Nigeria.|Obafemi Awolowo University, Agricultural Economics|1983|||OAU |||||xii, 178p.|||||||English||Obafemi Awolowo University, H. O. Library, Ile-Ife, Osun State. E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|Agriculture has always been an important single activity in the Nigerian economy and a prime mover of growth. However, some problems such as the rural exodusof the virile labour force, absence or poor physical infra-structural facilities, poor storage and marketing facilities, lack of credit and technical know-how have subjected the farmers particularly the small-holder to small scale production.To extricate the farmers from the unsatisfactory farming situation and help them break-out of the vicious cycle of poverty, disease and ignorance the state government established a number of institutions to serve them.One of such institutions is the Investment and Credit Corporation of Oyo State (ICCOS), the agricultural lending activities of which is the focus of this study. Granted that credit facility should be made available to small-scale farmer, however, determing the right amount of credit to give to a farmer-borrower is a problem to credit institutions because granting too little or too much credit may be counter productive.This study investigates the loan activities of the Investment and Credit Corporation of Oyo State. The organizational and operational structures of the agricultural credit department of the corporation as well as its effectiveness in agricultural loan administration were examined. Based on farmers' responses, the study highlights the need to grant adequate and timely loans to farmers, to simplify lending procedures and to create loan bases so as to encourage more farmers to benefit from credit services.It is recommended that granting of loan should be decentralized so that zonal officers can grant the small-holder farmer credit. The risk of infection developing in surgical wounds is dependent on a number of factorsincluding patient, personnel and the environment.	The study was undertaken to determine the importance of patients, personnel and airborne microbes in the operating room as sources of intraoperative contamination of clean wounds at the Obafemi Awolowo University Teaching Hospitals Complex, Ile-Ife.A total of one hundred and one patients were monitored for postsurgical clean wound infection in this study. Swabs of the anterior nares of patients and attending surgical staff were cultured on blood and mannitol salt agar, while samples of incision site of patients were obtained with sterile cotton tipped applicators prior to skin closure and plated on blood agar. Aerial contamination of the operating suite was assessed by exposing settle plates containing various bacteriological media. Inanimate items in the operating room were routinely swabbed and plated on blood agar. Bacterial isolates were identified by bacteriological methods, while fungal isolates were identified based only on their cultural and morphological characteristics. Phage typing of Staphylococcus aureus isolates 'recovered from patients and attending surgical staff was performed using the present international set of typing phages and the antibiotic sensitivity patterns of these strains were determined by the agar diffusion method.The incidence of clean wound infection in this study was 1.98% and a mortality rate of 2.97% was observed. The mean colony count per plate per hour (c. p. h) of microbes on settle plates ranged from 1.1 - 49.4 and 0.9 - 74.6 colonies in the operating room and exit area of the operating suite respectively. No correlation was found between the total number of bacteria and the incidence of postoperative clean wound infection. The nasal carriage rates of Staphylococcus aureus isolates among patients and attending surgical staff were 12.8% and 17.9% respectively. The phage typing of the Staphylococcus aureus isolates recovered from patients and attending surgical staff revealed that group III and non typeable strains were predominant. All the Staphylococcus aureus isolates were resistant to penicillin, while 55.6% were resistant to tetracycline.	In addition, 17.2% and 13.8% were resistant to chloramphenicol and streptomycin respectively. Sixteen percent of the wound swabs taken prior to skin closure showed bacterial growth but none of these wounds developed infection.The study revealed that coagulase negative staphylococci were the predominant organisms associated with clean wound colonization and that sources of. intraoperative bacterial contamination of the woundwere the patients microflora and the operating room air.|Theses collection|Postoperative wound infection/prolonged hospitalization of surgical patients/intraoperative contamination of clean wounds*||||||Adewumi  V. E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|In the years ahead, particularly the next decade, the secondary schools in Nigeria will face their greatest challenge in history. The challenge will arise because, unless some people are to be denied its benefits, secondary education must be developed and expanded far beyond anything in all of its previous history and present demands.Projections, surveys and well meaning comments already indicate that within the next few years, secondary school enrolments will be doubled, especially taking into account the present large number of Universal free primary education enrolees who, after graduating from the primary school will have to find avenues of continuing their education in the secondary schools. The implications of the projected secondary school enrolments are many, One of these is that plant and other physical facilities must not only be provided and expanded, but they, in addition, must be well operated, properly maintained, safely insured, and clearly accounted for.Before the envisaged demands are put on our secondary schools, this study has therefore explored and attempted to evaluate the present status of school plant management in 8 selected secondary grammar schools in the Ondo Local Schools Board area. Th. aim of this pioneering effort was to offer some suggestions, depending on the outcome of the study, not only on how to achieve quality education, but how to provide this quality education under adequate, safe learning conditions, and of course, at theleast possible financial cost. Additionally, the "lighthouse" effect of the study will help to draw the attention of school administrators, other officials and research workers to some rich areas of school plant management.School plant administration covers a broad area of activities, but this study has confined itself only to school property management - that is, the operation, maintenance, insurance and accounting services of school physical plants.In order to get some direct light on the selected aspects of school plant administration, an intensive excursion was first made into :elated literature on this subject. With this theoretical backdrop, survey questionnaires were constructed and later administered on school officials and some other personnel charged with the responsibility of caring for, and maintaining physical plants in the selected schools. Ideas were also drawn through personal interviews and on-the-spot observation of school documents and records to double-check and supplement some of the responses given in the survey-questionnaires. This approach, apart from providingfor objectivity, also endeavoured throughout to let the school administrators tell us what they knew and did about school plant management.Collected data were synthesized and treated descriptively. At certain points however, simple statistical methods involving the use of percentages and tables were introduced to throw more light on the findings of the studyThe consensus of the findings of the study demonstrated that not enough attention has been paid to the management of school plants. Resultant can be said that only some efforts have been made to preserve the large financial values on school physical facilities, and to provide enough learning places for students. This is in effect saying that while therewere some "meeting-points" between the theory and practice of school plant management, yet, it can still be further claimed that there were certain lapses and shortcomings as revealed by this study, in the actual practice of school plant administration. We were led to believe this conclusion primarily on the strength of the analyzed data which on the one hand indicated that all the schools he d a maintenance department each which employed some custodians for the care and safe-keeping of school plants,and that major repairs and renovations to school buildings and other physical facilities were performed on contract basis. At the same time, the data pointed out the fewness of custodial staff, lack of guiding custodial policies, absence of any clearly stated conditions of service for custodians, small percentage of maintenance expenditures, inadequacy of building safety-devices, unavailability of clear specifications and regulations for insuring school plants; and the irregular procedures for registering school physical resources.Some implications of the results of the study which had been fully documented somewhere in this report, made it almost mandatory for one to recommend ways and means of action, by which school administrators, ministries of education, and all others who are interested in the positive growth of education in this country, Nigeria,, can attempt to improve the present unenviable and slipshod manner of administering and managing the school physical resources.Aptly put, one is very convinced of the important role which the ministries of education can play in improving the standard of school plant management, more so, when the government has taken over the administration of the schools. Besides, being the major financier of the schools, the government is at a vantage position to cease this singular opportunity to impress it upon school administrators, the importance of proper school plant administration, after all, it is said, "he who pays the piper, dictates the tune".No doubt, the degree of concerted and consciencious efforts at implementing the recommended suggestions, may well prove a hallmark in history at providing safe learning environments for our children, and at achieving quality education in Nigeria.|Theses collection|SCHOOL PLANT MANAGEMENT PRACTICE/Ondo State Secondary Schools/safe learning environments*||||||Adeyanju Albert Sunday|Male|Marketing in  domestic banking  National Bank of Nigeria Limited in perspective||||||M. Almost all the samples fall below A line in the Casagrande's plasticity chart indicating that the materials are mainly inorganic silts and sands with little clay binders. However, it is shown that C.B.R values should be used with great caution since they do not always reflect the "worst" moisture condition for the subgrade material. The undrained shear strength for the undisturbed materials are higher than those for the compacted materials, probably reflecting the effect of soil structure on shear strength.Although the traffic level had significantly exceeded the design value, the causes of failure has been traced not necessarily to high traffic levelbut to poor surface and subsurface drainage conditions triggered and aggravated by slump materials from associated slope failures in the cut sections. It is shown that if road failures are to be averted equal emphasis must be placed on adequate slope design and protection, adequate surface and subsurface drainage as it is presently placed on adequate structural thickness and good compaction control in the current Nigerian Highway design practice. E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|The Kerri-Kerri formation is a flat-lying to gently dipping sedimentary sequence of rare basal conglomerate, grit, sandstones, silt and clay lying unconformably on older Maestrichtian sediments. The absence of marine fauna, its brown coloration, and the occasional upward fining of grain sizes indicate a continental deposit. The presence of the pollenSpinizonocolpites baculatus, Monocolpites marginatus and the absence of the Neogene volcanic plugs from the sequence suggest a humid, fresh water to near coastal deposits of possible Lower Paleocene to Upper Miocene age.A zonal grouping of zircon breadth-length plots, the broadly defined maxima of elongation frequency curves of the said mineral and the paucity of black zircons suggest an ill-sorted arkosic sandstone derived from several sources. The heavy minerals, kyanite, staurolite, tourmaline, euhedral aircon, rutile, authigenic limonite, abundant alterite4, rounded zircon indicatea derivation from a Basement Complex area associated with volcanic activities and from older sediments. An east-west and north-east moving average paleocurrent direction infers a geographical location of the basement west of the Kerri-Kerri depocentre.Rare basal conglomerate, isolated lowly pebbly ridges, ferruginous sandstone, bedded silt, shrinkage cracks and massive clay-supported grit, locally channel sandstone with thin horizons of coal and carbonaceous clay are characteristic of fluvial, deltaic and marginal lacustrine deposits.Asymmetrical ripple marks with ripple spacing between 12 + 1.5 cm and 5 cm and ripple indices usually less than 15 suggest both current and wave ripples in a low to high energy regime. Locally unidirectional, rapid and pulsational currents are inferred from the rhythmic graded beddings. Pseudo-rattle stone are evidence of less vegetation and carbon dioxide during its depositional history. A homogenous kaolinite clay reflects a single source area, lithology, weathering processes and dispersal pattern common to all the clays in the formation.|Theses collection|Sedimentary petrography/The Kerri-Kerri formation*||||||Agu Stella Ifeoma|Female|PHYTOCHEMICAL STUDIES ON THE VOLATILE PRODUCTS OF SOME PLANTS USEDLOCALLY IN THE TREATMENT OF SKIN DISEASES ||||||M . The exploitation of the labour of the apprentice helps to proliferate entrepreneur-ship in the furniture industry by a process of reaction through the emigration of disgusted men who want to establish their own workshop. The fluid (blood) is assumed incompressible and Newtonian and the wavelength of the generated wave is longer than the tube dimater   (/r > 1000, where r is the tube radius, h the wavelength)[13]. The flow velocity is small compared with the wave velocity[4,20]. The one-dimensional Navier-Stokes equation isused to consider inviscid flow in a uniform impermeable tube by the method of characteristics to obtain simple - wave solutions indicating time and the position where shock begins to form.In addition, a viscous flow in a tapered branching tube is considered. The efflux or fluid outflow per unit length of tube is accounted for by the function(p [2,4, 18] introdued into the continuity equation and its specific form later chosen in line with Skalak's condition [4](edited). E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|There is a subtle controversy an the appropriate strategy to be adopted in the development of the Nigerian Oil Palm industry in order to restore its produce back on the country's export profile, and at the same time provide enough quantity for domestic consumption. While some people feel that the most effective approach is at the rural level through a rehabilitation scheme of the natural palm groves, others believe that the best way-out is to evolve a vigorous plantation system for the crop. The protagonists of the second school of thought constantly point to the Malaysian experience in plantation practice through which production of this crop has progressed by leaps and bounds since 1939 and enabled those countries to supplant the Nigerian position as a leading world exporter of palm oil.The present study covering 1972-75 provides empirical evidence based on an exist-1g oil palm estate - Cowan Estate in Bendel State - to confirm or refute the view that an oil palm plantation can be operated on a profit basis in anylocality in Nigeria where favourable climatic and ecological conditions are available. Within the serious limitations imposed by the use of data from a secondary source, the problems of cost allocations between the estate and otherfarms which are operated under the same organization, and the seniscence of palms in the estate, the author employed three analytical techniques in the investigation, These techniques comprised the Gross Margin Analysis and the Internal Rate of Return for investigating the issue of financial profitability, and regression analysis for identifying the various resource factors that mostly influence profitability. The Gross Margin Analysis at current prices indicated a positive but fluctuating annual gross margins for the four year period under study; but when the technique was repeated under constant prices, an annual gross margin that steadily declined from year to year and actually became negative in the last two years was obtained. The Internal Rate of Return based on a salvage price of N300,000 which the Bendel State Government paid for the estate gave a high figure of 57% which could not be validated, and which in fact, fell to a low rate of 4.6% when the estimated low true economicinvestment value of the farm was used. Lastly, the Regression analysis on resource productivities revealed that the estate was operating at diminishing returns at the farm level but at increasing returns at the mill level, As operations at both levels fell within the "irrational stages"(stages III and I respectively) of economic theory, a further examination of the MVP/MFC ratios of the individual resource factors indicated that insufficient labour is being used at the farm level while the mill requires to be worked to greater capacity, both labour and capital-wise.The findings obtained through the Gross Margin Analysis show the distorting effect of instability of price on productivity. For the estate it was clear that the present profit earnings are more a function of inflationary prices than that of efficiency in resource-use, and the policy implication here is that management should cut its operational costs in order to enhance profitability. In the case of the findings that emerged from the application of IRR technique, the government investment decision in paying only a salvage value for a farm that has doubtful profitability prospects is justified. Lastly, the Regression Analysis elicited valuable findings for locating two operational centres at the estate and identifying areas in each case where more costs could be incurred in order to optimise profit, or where costs should be reduced in order to achieve an optimal profit goal. In total, there is no doubt that Cowan Estate can pay its way if resource factorsare properly managed. Thus if a plantation with very old trees like Cowan can hold out such hopes, there are bright profit prospects for oil palm plantations in Nigeria.|Theses collection|Oil Palm industry/Nigerian Oil Palm industry/oil plantation practice*||||||Adeniyi Funso|Male|STUDIES OF THE PHYSICO-CHEMICAL FACTORS AND THE PLANKTONIC ALGAE OF LAKE KAINJI, NIGERIA||||||PhD. The studies were carried out from January 1973 to September 1975 at eigth sampling stations chosen to represent the entire lake.Sampling programme and techniques were designed such that spatiotemporal variations in the factors might be revealed. The basic physical factors that were investigated included water temperature, transparency, turbidity, colour, wind-generated waves and sunshine duration. The data thus obtained were analysed to provide information on the thermal propertiesof the lake including its mixing/stratification patterns, stability, heat budgets and eddy conductivity; its optical properties of radiation terms, light-climate spectral composition and variations; and its hydromechanical factors including seiches, wave dimensionless functions and set-up.About twenty chemical factors including dissolved gases, nutritional salts, alkali metals, total and bicarbonate hardness, conductivity, hydrogen ion concentration, Biochemical Oxygen Demand and Organic matter were studied.In addition to the studies of the photosynthetic pigments and the taxonomy of the planktonic algae, data and information on the climate of the lake area and the hydrology of the lake itself were also obtained.Results showed that the physical limnology of the lake is determined or influenced mainly by its latitudinal position and hydrology. The lake i a warm monomictic type with high tropicality index, stability and annual amplitude of temperature variation but low light penetration characteristics .Although its wind and wave properties agreed, within limits, with the Sverdrup-Munk (1941) curves and models by the U.S. Army (1962) for large lakes and Oceans, actual wave conditions were uncorrespondingly low as borned out by the low significant wave-height observed .The lake is poor in chemical nutrients, and belongs both to the bicarbonate group of oligotrophic waters and class I of African waters according to Tailing and Tailing (1965) . Apart from having a relatively high proportion of magnesium, its stoichiometry and general properties are those typical of African waters .A systematic account including checklists and illustrated keys were provided on the algal flora which constisted  of about 360 taxa of 305 species, 98 genera, 35 families and five divisions.Algal standing crop was so high that it produced self-shading effect on light penetration and on the horizontal extent of the photosynthesis-depth profile thus giving low values of both e s (i.e . E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|Plant available P in the soil has frequently been evaluated using an empirical chemical extraction procedure. Recent research has shown that P diffuses through the soil to the plant root, so that a kinetic approach may improve the accuracy of measuring available P.In the present study, the relationships between P adsorption isotherms, P diffusion rate in soil, soil solution P concentration, plant root length, plant age, P uptake, and dry matter yield in the greenhouse, were investigated in five Indiana soils, using millet (Panicum milaceum) as the test crop. P uptake characteristics of millet were determined in solution culture at different ages. Using a mathematical model, P uptake was computed from the measured P properties of the soil, and the plant root characteristics; root-hair effect was not considered in the model. The result was compared with the observed P uptake in the greenhouse.The observed P uptake and P uptake rates in the greenhouse were much greater than the uptake and uptake rates computed for hair-less roots. Hence, the significance of root-hairs to P availability insoil was evaluated from observations on the root-hair cylinder volume.For a 10 day uptake period, P uptake from soil had much better correlation with total available P in the soil (r = 0.94) than withsoil solution P concentration (r = 0.68) or P diffusion rate (r = 0.4). However, as the P uptake time was increased to 17 days, the correlation with available P became poorer, while the correlation with each of the solution P and P diffusion rate improved significantly. P uptake from soil was directly related to root length, linearly during the earlier uptake periods (10 days), and curvilinearly later. Also, most of the available P within the root-hair cylinder in soil was available to the root for absorption within a few days. Hence, the high correlation between P uptake and the available P during the first 10 days of uptake, during which time the P diffusion rate was not a limiting factor of P availability to the root surface. This would explain the good correlation often obtained between P uptake and Bray P in soil. For a considerably extended uptake period however (17 days), P diffusion rate from the bulk soil to root surface became a significant limiting factor of P availability because P has had enough time to move to the root surface from considerable distance in the bulk soil, and because the available P within root-hair cylinder of the older segment of the root has been largely depleted. Because of the delayed influence of P diffusion rate on P availability to root surface, P buffering capacity of the soil also had no significant relationship with P uptake up to 14 days, but did have a good correlation when uptake period increased to 17 days.|Theses collection|plant root/kinetic process*||||||Adesanya Saburi Adejimi|Male|THE BIOLOGICAL STANDARDISATION OF THE ROOT OF SOME ZANTHOXYLUM SPECIES (FAGARA SPP.) This reorganization raised several questions such as:(1)	Should the Institute be an integral part of the StateUniversity Faculty of Agriculture?(2)	Should it be a separate unit in the University on equal basis with the Faculty?(3)	Should it be a complete autonomous organization outside of the University and the Ministry?(4)	How should its activities be coordinated with those of related agencies?The solution adopted by the State government was to accept the second alternative referred to above. The Institute was given theresponsibility of coordinating its own activities with those of the State University Faculty of Agriculture and the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources.MethodThe data were collected from staff members of the Institute, Faculty and Ministry described above, using questionnaires and personally conducted interviews. A review of official records and documents provided additional information on organizational policies and procedures. The data were analyzed through descriptive statistics such as percentages, medians, and chi-square.FindingsA limited number and degree of cooperative relationships were reported in research, teaching and extension related activities. A greater number and extent of competition and conflicts developed over personnel, appropriation of state funds and materials, and jurisdiction over agricultural research and instruction. Most of the cooperative efforts reported among the organizations occurred prior to the transfer of the research and training functions of the Ministry to the University; whereas competition and conflicts became apparent with the advent of this reorganization.Three factors appeared to be most influential in the kind of relationships found among the agencies. These included: (1) the quality of organizational leadership, (2) centralization of decision making, and (3) utilization of individual as contrasted to team performance as criterion for staff evaluation and reward. Otherfactors that showed relationships with interorganizational acts were (1) length of service, (2) official status, (3) benefits to individual participants, and (4) the extent to which agency members perceived each other favorably.RecommendationsIt was recommended that the organizational leaders and policy makers take actions to:1.	Minimize disagreements among the agencies by substituting conflict situations with a more impersonal problem-solving approach;2.	Encourage team efforts as basis for evaluation and reward through (1) redefinition of roles and criteria for evaluation, and (2) joint appointments;3.	Encourage close contacts among staff members through seminars, conferences etc. and circulation of research reports;4.	Establish a joint committee of the three agencies to disburse funds and supervise joint programs and give it the power for demanding conformance from its member agencies; and5.	Encourage further research into additional organizational and leadership characteristics that might influence inter-organizational relationships.|Theses collection|Agricultural research, teaching and extension in the Western State of Nigeria*||||||Amadi Emmanuel Azunda|Male|THE DEVEL0PMENT OF HUMAN RESOURCES IN THE CIVIL SERVICE 	OF THE RIVERS STATE OF NIGERIA                      ||||||MPA Public Administration|R. The paucity of executive capacity in the Rivers State Civil Service is traceable to the long-term neglect of the development of the local manpower by the former government of the area. Added to this was the destructive effect of the civil war which considerably damaged the economy of the State. Above all, the State Government embarked upon massive economic development programmes soon after its inception, for the ultimate improvement of the life of the people.The successful implementation of these programmes presupposes an efficient and committed manpower component of the plans, anda management style that could train, motivate and fully utilizethe available personnel. The treatments were P0, P11 P2, P3 corresponding to 0, 40, 80 160 ppm P applied as NaH2PO4 2H20 in the greenhouse and single superphosphate in the field, and Mg0, Mg1, Mg2, Mg3 corresponding to 0, 20, 40, 80 ppm Mg applied as MgC12.6H20 in the greenhouse and MgSO4 in the field.The amount of available Mg recovered from the soil did not seem to be affected by the level of P aFC:\WINDOWS\hinhem.scrpplied. Neither was available P recovered from the soil affected by Mg applied.However, the generally low P recovery observed in this investigation could be attributed partially to the clay content of the soils used and also to the level of Mg as a cation applied in the treatments which could probably give rise to P-Mg complexes.The results of the greenhouse experiment show that plant
height was increased with increasing levels of P with the maximumat 40 ppm P beyond which further P-fertilization depressed tomato plant height. The minus P treatments (control) produced generally poor plant growth. Plant height was improved generally with increasing levels of Mg. As a result of this, policy makers have always been concerned with the development, training and utilization of skilled manpower.Basically, this study examined the training, employment and job performance of vocational school graduates in Bendel State of Nigeria. The main purpose was to analyse and evaluate the State's vocational school programme in terms of training, employment and job performance. It was based on the hypothesis that there is no difference between the characteristics of manpower produced in Bendel State's vocational schools and those of the manpower required in the State's labour market in terms of training, number, employment and job performance. From this hypothesis, six operational hypotheses were formulated to guide the study.To test the six operational hypotheses, five instruments,Head of Department Questionnaire (HDQ), Principal Questionnaire (PQ), Personnel Manager Questionnaire (PMQ), Graduate Questionnaire (GQ), and Foreman Questionnaire (FQ) were constructed to collect data. The responses obtained through the five instruments were analysed by statistical methods which yielded the following main results:(1)	the level of skills required by employers was significantly higher than the level of skills imparted to students in the State's vocational schools;(2)	the number of graduates trained in the State's vocational schools (1974-1977) was higher than the number of such graduates employed in the State during the same period;(1)	(3)	the number of students enrolled in the State's vocational schools in classes I, II and III in 1978 for exceeded the number of jobs available for vocational school graduates during the 1978-80 forecast period;(4)	differences existed in demand for the different categories of vocational school graduates in the state;(5)	the expected maximum level of job performance was significantly higher than the actual level of job performance by the vocational school graduates in the State; and,(6)	a significant difference existed in job performance among the graduates from the different vocational schools in the State.The findings led to the following conclusions:(a)	that the standard of training in the State vocational schools was low and that the low standard was due mainly to the lack of information from the employers of labour on the standard expected of the graduates in the labour market;(b)	that there was unemployment problem (41 percent) among the vocational school graduates in the State, particularly among graduates in auto-mechanics, electrical trades and telecommunications; and,(c)	that the level of job performance by the vocational school graduates from the State vocational schools was low mainly because of the poor standard of training in the vocational schools.The study concluded that for the vocational training system to be able to contribute to the production of competent skilled manpower so urgently needed in the State, several steps must be taken. The steps include manpower surveys, formulation of government policies, drawing up of plans for implementation, establishment of a State advisory council on vocational education, vocational curriculum development and provision of adequate funds and equipment. Further, the studyemphasized the need for streamlined admission policies and practices, training of technical education teachers and the establishment of industrial training within a legislative frame work. ||||||PhD. Education|W. P. Anderson||New York, USA|Columbia University, Teachers College|1973||||||||299p.|||||||English||Obafemi Awolowo University, H. O. Library, Ile-Ife, Osun State. E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|The purpose of this study was to investigate Nigeria's educational achievement between 1945 and 1970 vis-a-sis projections in educational plans during the same period. Specifically, the study intended first to investigate the extent to which there has been a variance between stated quantitative goals and actual educational development in Nigeria; and second, to explore factors that made for the variance between educational plans and actual educational development,The methodology consisted of three major steps. First the researcher examined Nigeria's educational plans since 1945 and identified their quantitative goals and the priorities stressed. Second, Nigeria's actual educational development was analysed and the degree to which quantitative objectives and priorities indicated in the plans conformed or varied with the actual educational development wasdetermined and compared. Finally, using governmental and non-governmental reports, and research in the social sciences which deal with problems relevant to the Nigerian experience, the reasons for discrepancies between educational plans and actual educational development were explored and discussed.The examination of the planned and actual quantitative development showed that first, the quantitative targets set forth in the plans have not been achieved, and second, that the orders of priority implicit in the verbal statements of the plans have in fact been inverted during the process of implementation. There was glaring overspending in the area of primary education which was also acclaimed top priority during the process of implementation contradicting the conditions set for such development in the plans. With primary education consuming a huge proportion of the education funds, the achievement of actual priorities in other areas was already hampered. The areas that suffered most, upper secondary and teacher education, are the areas identified as priorities in the plans. Higher education, not considered a top priority in the plans, also came to the forefront during the process of implementation and combined with primary education to eclipse the much acclaimed intermediate education as represented by upper secondary and middle-level teacher education. In all the levels of education, large-scale quantitative short falls were indicated.In seeking an explanation to this phenomenon in Nigeria'seducational planning experience, certain conditions conventionally associated with the success of educational plans were identified. For a plan to succeed in achieving the priorities and quantitative targets it sets for itself, one or more of the following conditions must be present:1.	The political conditions in the planning country must be, by common judgement, conducive to smooth and unhampered implementation.2.	The technical and political aspects of the plan should be clearly determined and the fusion of the two is avoided.3.	The financial resources for sustaining the plan must be adequate or based on realistic and definite expectations.4.	An implementation machinery exists or a formal implementation machinery is legally or officially recognized.5.	The statistics upon which the plan is developed are reliable and capable of producing desired results.It was shown that the variance between educational plans and what actually happened, and the non-realization of the quantitative goals ofthe plans resulted largely from Nigeria's failure to meet these conditions.|Theses collection|Nigeria's educational achievement between 1945 and 1970/projections in educational plans/stated quantitative goals and actual educational development in Nigeria/factors that made for the variance between educational plans and actual educational development*||||||Yakubu Henrietta M.|Male|UNITED STATES/CHILEAN RELATIONS: THE DYNAMICS OF DEPENDENCY||||||M. This hypothesis is examined throughout the study, but more specifically in Chapter Four where it is sought to be verified through the cases analysed.The major findings of this study include the following:(a) the decision-making process at Ibadan hasbeen largely modelled on the British provincial university system which relies heavily on collective decision-making in committees or boards;b) the power of the Visitor to the university grew considerably from 1966 onwards, and such power was used from time to time as the need arose.(c)	the incidence of violent student unrest resulted always in the externalisation of purely internal university issues which in turn provided the justification for Government's intervention;(d)	the Government has strengthened its control over university affairs through such central agencies as the National Universities Commission (NUC) and the Joint Admission and Matriculation Board (JAMB);(e)	from 1974 onwards, the University has come to depend almost totally on subventions from the Government. This has consequently motivated the latter to exert more or less absolute control over every aspect of university life;(f)	the internal decision-making process through the committee system has continued all the time; it has survived mainly because the majority of academics seemed to believe in its inherent virtues. This belief is based upon the rationality of the process through discussion, and its democratic nature.This study follows a general plan, It begins by discussing the university as a formally organised institution within the context of the theory and practice of the decision-making process. Individual, and collective decision-making theory is generally discussed,while university decision-making is examined in some detail. Then, the structure and processes in the University of Ibadan are critically analysed and the symbiotic relationship between the Government and the University is described, using the systems concept to illustrate the nature of such a relationship. There-after, some specific cases are analysed in order to show that the Government exercised direct control over the university from time to time and was actively influencing the internal university decision-making process.The conclusion to the study is in two parts: the first part briefly discusses the findings of a questionnaire designed to elicit opinion amongst those who have participated in formulating and implementing collective decisions at Ibadan. It was found that the majority believe in the committee system as a democratic method of taking collective decisions in spite of the constant threat of external interference. The second part discusses ways and means of restoring university autonomy, and of improving the relations between the Governmentand the university. These include a review of the powers of the Visitor or the abolition of the post; a review of the powers and functions of the NUC; a re-examination of the provisions of the JAMB. The reality of student power oughtto be acknowledged and such energies should be channelled to productive purposes by deliberately encouraging participation in policy matters at the appropriate levels. Form||Ile-Ife, Nigeria|Obafemi Awolowo University, Sociology|1967|||OAU|||||225p.|||||||English||Obafemi Awolowo University, H. O. Library, Ile-Ife, Osun State. E-mail: ul@library.oauife.edu.ng|http://library.oauife.edu.ng||||I hereby authorize the Hezekiah Oluwasanmi Library to copy my thesis in whole or in part in response to request from individual researchers and organizations for the purpose of private study or research|This dissertation was designed to investigate the social factors that condition the satisfactions and dissatisfactions that the Nigerian industrial worker derives from his occupational activity. To the extent that the self arises and functions and is maintained through continued interaction with significant others in a symbolic social situation, this study attempted to delimit these significant others and to identify their social locations in order to determine their influence on worker satisfaction. The interactive situation ineluctably predisposes the worker to certain invidious comparisons with significant-others or reference group members, and the results of such comparisons predispose the worker to certain pat-terns of satisfactory or dissatisfactory behavior. The central organizing hypothesis for this study, therefore, is that reference group comparisons are significantly related to worker satisfaction. In addition, the study sought to investigate the shifting sources of reference group selection. The contention is that the selection of comparativereference groups is not random but shifts with the processes of industrialization and socialization into the industrial work force. There is a tendency to select congruent reference groups in order to engender effective industrial socialization and commitment. At the initial phase, ex-tended kin members located in non-industrial occupations are selected as reference group members, while at the intermediate phase, extended kin members in industrial occupations are selected. At the final phase, non-kin members in industrial occupations are selected as reference group members. This pattern of selection serves the function of reinforcing socialization.A sample of 141 Nigerian factory workers from four factories--Metal Containers, Metal Construction, both of which are located in Lagos, the capital of the Federal Re-public of Nigeria, Nigerian Bottling Company and Lafia Canning Factory, both of which are located in Ibadan, the regional capital of Western Nigeria--were interviewed in 1965. The sample does not represent a random sample of all factory workers in Nigeria. The selection of a non-random sample of the total work force was due primarily to the problems of gaining entry into other factories to legitimize the study. The sample, however, represents a random sample of the four factories in this study. The interview schedule utilized both forced-choice and open-ended questions and no attempt was made to use composite indices becauseof the difficulties of constructing theoretically valid measures for such indices. Contingency distributions constituted the main statistical tool of analysis, while statistical significance was ascertained by means of chi-square and derivative tests. Significance level was set at the conventional .05 level.The investigation of workers' attitudes in each of the areas of worker satisfaction--sector, occupation, and work--confirmed the general hypothesis that worker satisfaction is significantly related to worker comparisons with reference group members. Furthermore, it confirmed the proposition that satisfaction is not an overall entity but represents discrete clusters of attitudes and feelings. Satisfaction was found to increase with expanding spheres of social activity. Thus workers tended to be less satisfied with their work tasks than with their occupations, and less satisfied with their occupations than with their sectors of employment.A major substantive finding was that Nigerian factory workers were relatively satisfied with their sectorof employment and their occupation but relatively less satisfied with their specific work tasks. However, even this relative dissatisfaction with work tasks represents a fairly healthy sign when the recency of industrial development and the attendant difficulties of adjustment are taken into account.Besides comparisons with reference group members, skill level (which represents degrees of socialization into industrial life) and urban residence appear to be the major variables that condition sector and occupational satisfaction, while work control and the recognition of the worker's performance by his boss condition work satisfaction. Variables like the performance of traditional obligations such as help to members of the kinship group and demographic variables such as age, education, marital status, number of children and number of wives, were not found to be related to work satisfaction. Skill level, though related to sector and occupational satisfaction, was not found to be related to work satisfaction. Semi-skilled workers were found to be more satisfied with work tasks than skilled workers, and skilled workers slightly more satisfied than unskilled workers. However, in the areas of sector and occupational satisfaction, it was found that satisfaction increased with skill level.The sector of employment of the worker's father appears to be significantly related to the choice of the preferred sector of employment of the industrial worker. Those whose fathers were employed in non-agricultural sec-tors are found to be more likely to select non-agricultural sectors of employment than those workers whose fathers were in the agricultural sector.Significant differences exist among the fourfactories. In addition to the fact that they were established at different times--Metal Containers in 1940, Lafia Canning in 1954, Metal Construction in 1958 and Nigerian Bottling in 1962--significant differences were found in the mean age of the workers, marital status, rural-urban residence, skill level, job control, education, patterns of reference group comparisons and worker satisfaction. There were also differences in management policies toward conditions of work and also in union organization. Finally, in addition to the viability of reference group concept in analyzing worker satisfaction, the study also found a shifting pattern in the selection of reference group members. This pattern followed the degrees of socialization into industrial work. Unskilled workers tend to choose extended kin members in non-industrial occupations, while skilled workers chose extended kin-members in industrial occupations, as reference group members.|Theses collection|Nigerian Factory Workers/ worker satisfaction*||||||Asante Kingsley Kofi|Male||||||||||Ile-Ife, Nigeria|Obafemi Awolowo University,||||OAU||||||||||||English||Obafemi Awolowo University, H.
